	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>How do borders echo and reverberate as cultural geographies, unsettling space and forcing bodies to move, to organize, and to perform? How do performers and scholars account for and navigate their bordered existence, when traversing them can regularly (re)produce the conditions for both precarious and secure living? What conditions arise amongst bodies, boundaries, and the spaces there in between? The 2018 Department of Performance Studies Graduate Student Conference, In Motion: Performance and Unsettling Borders, invites graduate students&#8212;practitioners and scholars&#8212;to generate dialogue and debate by coming together around artistic work and interdisciplinary thinking.</p>			</div>

	<meta property="og:description" content="Pediatrics Grand Rounds are held every Friday morning from 8:00 - 9:00 a.m. in the eleventh floor conference center at Ann &amp;amp; Robert H. Lurie Children's Hospital of Chicago. Distinguished Northwestern University faculty present, along with other nationwide experts, on various subject areas.
Recent topics have included Resident Simulation Training, Adolescent Obesity, Organ Donation, and HPV Vaccination.
Accreditation Statement
Northwestern University Feinberg School of Medicine is accredited by the Accreditation Council for Continuing Medical Education to provide continuing medical education for physicians.
Credit Hour Statement
Northwestern University Feinberg School of Medicine designates this live educational activity for a maximum of 1 AMA PRA Category 1 Credits &amp;#8482;. Physicians should only claim credit commensurate with the extent of their participation in the activity.
Grand Rounds Learning Objectives
At the conclusion of this activity, participants will be able to:
Discuss current diagnostic process for given disease presented. List treatment strategies for disease process being presented. Discuss pathophysiology for given disease presented. Identify and address safety and quality issues impacting management and outcome of the case presented.
Faculty disclosure
Speaker disclosures will be made available in writing and orally at each Grand Rounds session. The Course Director, Dr. Jennifer Trainor, has nothing to disclose. FSM&amp;#8217;s CME Leadership and Staff have nothing to disclose: John X. Thomas, Jr., PhD, Sr. Associate Dean for Medical Education, Genevieve Napier, CME Director, CME Project Specialists, Jen Banys, Michelle Mallery &amp;amp; Tristan Brown." />

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Pediatrics Grand Rounds are held every Friday morning from 8:00 - 9:00 a.m. in the eleventh floor conference center at Ann &amp; Robert H. Lurie Children's Hospital of Chicago. Distinguished Northwestern University faculty present, along with other nationwide experts, on various subject areas.</p>
<p>Recent topics have included Resident Simulation Training, Adolescent Obesity, Organ Donation, and HPV Vaccination.</p>
<p><strong>Accreditation Statement</strong></p>
<p>Northwestern University Feinberg School of Medicine is accredited by the Accreditation Council for Continuing Medical Education to provide continuing medical education for physicians.</p>
<p><strong>Credit Hour Statement</strong></p>
<p>Northwestern University Feinberg School of Medicine designates this live educational activity for a maximum of 1 AMA PRA Category 1 Credits &#8482;. Physicians should only claim credit commensurate with the extent of their participation in the activity.</p>
<p><strong>Grand Rounds Learning Objectives</strong></p>
<p>At the conclusion of this activity, participants will be able to:</p>
<p>Discuss current diagnostic process for given disease presented. List treatment strategies for disease process being presented. Discuss pathophysiology for given disease presented. Identify and address safety and quality issues impacting management and outcome of the case presented.</p>
<p><strong>Faculty disclosure</strong></p>
<p>Speaker disclosures will be made available in writing and orally at each Grand Rounds session. The Course Director, Dr. Jennifer Trainor, has nothing to disclose. FSM&#8217;s CME Leadership and Staff have nothing to disclose: John X. Thomas, Jr., PhD, Sr. Associate Dean for Medical Education, Genevieve Napier, CME Director, CME Project Specialists, Jen Banys, Michelle Mallery &amp; Tristan Brown.</p>			</div>

<p><strong>Saturday, April 28 </strong></p>
<p>9:00 &#8211; 9:30am: Breakfast/coffee</p>

<p>10:35 &#8211; 11:35am: Caitlin Fitchett, &#8220;Against Epistemic Partiality in Friendship&#8221; Comments by Nate Lauffer (NU)</p>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>At NUPOC, science, technology and art intersect. In recognition of the artistry that is vital to prosthetics and orthotics, NUPOC is delighted to announce the installation and exhibition of 5 abstracts in acrylic by Lee Oberlander.&#160;</p>
<p>Lee Oberlander&#160;derives inspiration from the world around him&#160;and distills his experience in abstracts of acrylic, pastel, encaustic, mixed media and collage.&#160; His vibrant work&#160;reflects&#160;the colors of nature and&#160;meditative Eastern philosophies. He has studied and exhibited at the Art Institute of Chicago, the Chicago Botanic Garden, Lillstreet Art Center and The Evanston Art Center.&#160;</p>			</div>

	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 Why Do Animal Studies?: The Turn to the The Turn to the &quot;Quasi-, Post-, Anti-, Non-, Para-&quot;</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />
	<link rel="shortcut icon" href="/favicon.ico" type="image/x-icon" />
	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/532840" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="Why Do Animal Studies?: The Turn to the The Turn to the &quot;Quasi-, Post-, Anti-, Non-, Para-&quot; 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="Why Do Animal Studies?: The Turn to the Quasi-, Post-, Anti-, Non-, Para- (QPANP) is a collaboration between the Animal/Nonhuman Workshop at the University of Chicago and the Environmental Humanities Research Workshop at Northwestern University. It is a reprise of a symposium that the Animal/Nonhuman Workshop (then the Animal Studies Workshop) hosted in 2014 with a similar title. At the time, everyone was doing Animal Studies: the eponymous question would have been inflected very differently. The reprise, as we have currently conceived of it, responds to a change in the objects of inquiry of the people and publications at the forefront of our subfield, from singular animals to communities, hybrids, machines, apocalypse, inorganic/nonliving things, virtual animals and others, large scale, deep time, energies, plants, and speculative and non-Earth worlds. The conference seeks, among other things, to assemble these objects into five semi-liquid categories: the quasi-, post-, anti-, non-, and para- animal. In so doing, it makes (one) sense of the new direction of our subfield. It is our grand aspiration that people will leave the conference with a sense of the questions to be asking at this moment in Animal/Nonhuman Studies and the types of objects (literary, artistic, scientific, and so on) that people are using to address them.
The conference includes plenary performances by the contemporary art and bioengineer Adam Zaretsky and the Antiguan-American author Jamaica Kincaid.
Why Do Animal Studies? is made possible with the generous support of The Kaplan Insitute for the Humanities, the Weinberg College of Arts and Sciences, the Buffett Institute for Global Studies, the Department of English, the Department of African American Studies, the Program in Critical Theory, and the Department of Art, Theory, and Practice at Northwestern University, and the Division of the Humanities, the Council on Advanced Studies, Global Studies at the College, and the Department of English at the University of Chicago.&amp;#160;" />
	<meta property="og:site_name" content="Northwestern University Events" />

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Why Do Animal Studies?: The Turn to the Quasi-, Post-, Anti-, Non-, Para- (QPANP) is a collaboration between the Animal/Nonhuman Workshop at the University of Chicago and the Environmental Humanities Research Workshop at Northwestern University. It is a reprise of a symposium that the Animal/Nonhuman Workshop (then the Animal Studies Workshop) hosted in 2014 with a similar title. At the time, everyone was doing Animal Studies: the eponymous question would have been inflected very differently. The reprise, as we have currently conceived of it, responds to a change in the objects of inquiry of the people and publications at the forefront of our subfield, from singular animals to communities, hybrids, machines, apocalypse, inorganic/nonliving things, virtual animals and others, large scale, deep time, energies, plants, and speculative and non-Earth worlds. The conference seeks, among other things, to assemble these objects into five semi-liquid categories: the quasi-, post-, anti-, non-, and para- animal. In so doing, it makes (one) sense of the new direction of our subfield. It is our grand aspiration that people will leave the conference with a sense of the questions to be asking at this moment in Animal/Nonhuman Studies and the types of objects (literary, artistic, scientific, and so on) that people are using to address them.</p>
<p>The conference includes plenary performances by the contemporary art and bioengineer Adam Zaretsky and the Antiguan-American author Jamaica Kincaid.</p>
<p>Why Do Animal Studies? is made possible with the generous support of The Kaplan Insitute for the Humanities, the Weinberg College of Arts and Sciences, the Buffett Institute for Global Studies, the Department of English, the Department of African American Studies, the Program in Critical Theory, and the Department of Art, Theory, and Practice at Northwestern University, and the Division of the Humanities, the Council on Advanced Studies, Global Studies at the College, and the Department of English at the University of Chicago.&#160;</p>			</div>

	<a class="detail-button" href="https://www.whydoanimalstudies.com/registration" target="_blank">Register</a>

		<a href="/calendar/4311" target="_blank">Environmental Humanities Research Workshop</a></p>

		
		<p><span class="label">Category: </span>
		<span class="event_category category_1_txt">Academic</span>

					<p><a href="http://www.whydoanimalstudies.com/" target="_blank">More Info</a></p>
			</div>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Why Do Animal Studies?: The Turn to the Quasi-, Post-, Anti-, Non-, Para- (QPANP) is a collaboration between the Animal/Nonhuman Workshop at the University of Chicago and the Environmental Humanities Research Workshop at Northwestern University. It is a reprise of a symposium that the Animal/Nonhuman Workshop (then the Animal Studies Workshop) hosted in 2014 with a similar title. At the time, everyone was doing Animal Studies: the eponymous question would have been inflected very differently. The reprise, as we have currently conceived of it, responds to a change in the objects of inquiry of the people and publications at the forefront of our subfield, from singular animals to communities, hybrids, machines, apocalypse, inorganic/nonliving things, virtual animals and others, large scale, deep time, energies, plants, and speculative and non-Earth worlds. The conference seeks, among other things, to assemble these objects into five semi-liquid categories: the quasi-, post-, anti-, non-, and para- animal. In so doing, it makes (one) sense of the new direction of our subfield. It is our grand aspiration that people will leave the conference with a sense of the questions to be asking at this moment in Animal/Nonhuman Studies and the types of objects (literary, artistic, scientific, and so on) that people are using to address them.</p>
<p>The conference includes&#160;plenary performances by the contemporary art and bioengineer Adam Zaretsky and the Antiguan-American author Jamaica Kincaid.</p>
<p>Why Do Animal Studies? is made possible with the generous support of The Kaplan Insitute for the Humanities, the Weinberg College of Arts and Sciences, the Buffett Institute for Global Studies, the Department of English, the Department of African American Studies, the Program in Critical Theory, and the Department of Art, Theory, and Practice at Northwestern University, and the Division of the Humanities, the Council on Advanced Studies, Global Studies at the College, and the Department of English at the University of Chicago.&#160;</p>			</div>

	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 Experiments in Form: Sam Gilliam, Alan Shields, Frank Stella</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />
	<link rel="shortcut icon" href="/favicon.ico" type="image/x-icon" />
	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/516288" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="Experiments in Form: Sam Gilliam, Alan Shields, Frank Stella 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="January 13 - June 24, 2018Katz Gallery
To celebrate the recent gift of the painting One (1970), by American artist Sam Gilliam (b. 1933), the Block Museum will present a focused exhibition of works by artists engaged with abstraction and the expansion of painting in the U.S. in the 1960s and 1970s. Donated from the estate of Dawn Clark Netsch from the Collection of Walter A. Netsch and Dawn Clark Netsch, One is a quintessential example of Gilliam&amp;#8217;s innovative &amp;#8220;drape&amp;#8221; paintings, which the artist began making in the late 1960&amp;#8217;s. Moving beyond the experiments of other painters of the era, Gilliam saturated raw, unstretched canvas with acrylic to create works that lie at the intersection of painting and sculpture. Born in Tupelo, Mississippi and raised in Louisville, Kentucky, Gilliam has been based in Washington D.C. since the early 1960&amp;#8217;s, and is part of a generation of Washington-based painters who have explored the boundaries of color, scale, and shape in painting.
One will be considered in the context of works from the Block&amp;#8217;s collection by Gilliam&amp;#8217;s contemporaries Alan Shields and Frank Stella. These works will be supplemented by additional Gilliam works drawn local collections.
Image: Sam Gilliam, Rim (detail), 1970 Oil and epoxy on un-stretched canvas 61 1/2 &amp;#215; 117 1/4 in. Collection of Smart Museum of Art, Gift of Debra and Robert N. Mayer from the Robert B. Mayer Memorial Loan Collection, 1983.45
&amp;#160;" />

		<span class="event_category category_2_txt">Fine Arts</span>
		</p>

					<p><a href="http://www.blockmuseum.northwestern.edu/view/exhibitions/upcoming-exhibitions/experiments-in-forms-sam-gilliam,-alan-shields,-frank-stella.html" target="_blank">More Info</a></p>
			</div>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>January 13 - June 24, 2018<br />Katz Gallery</p>
<p>To celebrate the recent gift of the painting One (1970), by American artist Sam Gilliam (b. 1933), the Block Museum will present a focused exhibition of works by artists engaged with abstraction and the expansion of painting in the U.S. in the 1960s and 1970s. Donated from the estate of Dawn Clark Netsch from the Collection of Walter A. Netsch and Dawn Clark Netsch, One is a quintessential example of Gilliam&#8217;s innovative &#8220;drape&#8221; paintings, which the artist began making in the late 1960&#8217;s. Moving beyond the experiments of other painters of the era, Gilliam saturated raw, unstretched canvas with acrylic to create works that lie at the intersection of painting and sculpture. Born in Tupelo, Mississippi and raised in Louisville, Kentucky, Gilliam has been based in Washington D.C. since the early 1960&#8217;s, and is part of a generation of Washington-based painters who have explored the boundaries of color, scale, and shape in painting.</p>
<p>One will be considered in the context of works from the Block&#8217;s collection by Gilliam&#8217;s contemporaries Alan Shields and Frank Stella. These works will be supplemented by additional Gilliam works drawn local collections.</p>
<p><em>Image: Sam Gilliam, Rim (detail), 1970 Oil and epoxy on un-stretched canvas 61 1/2 &#215; 117 1/4 in. Collection of Smart Museum of Art, Gift of Debra and Robert N. Mayer from the Robert B. Mayer Memorial Loan Collection, 1983.45</em></p>
<p>&#160;</p>			</div>

	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 Hank Willis Thomas: Unbranded</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />
	<link rel="shortcut icon" href="/favicon.ico" type="image/x-icon" />
	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/527629" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="Hank Willis Thomas: Unbranded 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="A practicing artist for over fifteen years, Hank Willis Thomas (American, b. 1976) has consistently explored American consumer culture, particularly as it relates to African-American subjects. His projects often appropriate imagery drawn from advertising campaigns to investigate the subtle and not so subtle ways in which this influential imagery reproduces and reinforces ideas about race and race relations. Hank Willis Thomas: Unbranded will consist of a tightly focused selection of approximately 40 photographs from two related bodies of works&amp;#8212;the 2005-08 series Unbranded: Reflections in Black by Corporate America and the 2015 series Unbranded: A Century of White Women 1915-2015, which draw directly from the visual repertoire of American print advertising.
Unbranded: Reflections in Black by Corporate America explores fifty years of print advertising targeted towards African-Americans&amp;#8212;from 1968, a year of heightened social and political protest that saw the assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr., until 2008, the year of the election of the first African American president. Thomas digitally stripped these advertisements of all text, including product names and slogans, allowing the impact of their images to be felt more acutely.
Unbranded: A Century of White Women 1915-2015 ends with the year in which Thomas finished working on the series, and stretches back to five years before American women gained the right to vote. Like Reflections in Black, the advertising images that are featured in A Century of White Women are stripped of text, heightening our awareness of how we read them as assertions about beauty, desire, virtue, and ideal white femininity.
Seen together, the works in these two series offer a unique opportunity to explore the ways in which Thomas interrogates images across subject matter and allows for a complex and nuanced contemplation of the interrelated construction of narratives about race, gender, and class through the vehicle of advertising. By honing in on print advertising, especially drawn from magazines, Thomas also provides an opportunity to reflect on the important role magazines played as a primary form of mass communication during the 20th century.
-----------------
About the Artist:
Hank Willis Thomas earned his BFA in photography and Africana studies from New York University in 1998 and MFA in photography/ MA in visual criticism from California College of Arts, San Francisco in 2004. He is a 2017 recipient of the Open Society Foundations&amp;#8217; Soros Equality Fellowship awarded to practitioners from a variety fields to support work that advances racial justice. He has been a W.E.B. DuBois Institute Resident Fellow at Harvard University, and has received awards and residencies from John Hopkins University, Headlands and Cit&amp;#233; Internationale Universitaire de Paris among others.
----------
Hank Willis Thomas: Unbranded is curated by Janet Dees, Steven and Lisa Munster Tananbaum Curator of Modern and Contemporary Art, with assistance from Tamar Kharatishvili, 2017-18 Block Graduate Curatorial Fellow. Funding for this exhibition has been provided by the David C. &amp;amp; Sarajean Ruttenberg Arts Foundation and the Robert Mapplethorpe Foundation.&amp;#160;This program is partially supported by a grant from the Illinois Arts Council Agency.
&amp;#160;" />

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>A practicing artist for over fifteen years, Hank Willis Thomas (American, b. 1976) has consistently explored American consumer culture, particularly as it relates to African-American subjects. His projects often appropriate imagery drawn from advertising campaigns to investigate the subtle and not so subtle ways in which this influential imagery reproduces and reinforces ideas about race and race relations. <strong><em>Hank Willis Thomas: Unbranded</em></strong> will consist of a tightly focused selection of approximately 40 photographs from two related bodies of works&#8212;the 2005-08 series <em>Unbranded: Reflections in Black by Corporate America</em> and the 2015 series <em>Unbranded: A Century of White Women 1915-2015</em>, which draw directly from the visual repertoire of American print advertising.</p>
<p><em>Unbranded: Reflections in Black by Corporate America</em> explores fifty years of print advertising targeted towards African-Americans&#8212;from 1968, a year of heightened social and political protest that saw the assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr., until 2008, the year of the election of the first African American president. Thomas digitally stripped these advertisements of all text, including product names and slogans, allowing the impact of their images to be felt more acutely.</p>
<p><em>Unbranded: A Century of White Women 1915-2015</em> ends with the year in which Thomas finished working on the series, and stretches back to five years before American women gained the right to vote. Like Reflections in Black, the advertising images that are featured in A Century of White Women are stripped of text, heightening our awareness of how we read them as assertions about beauty, desire, virtue, and ideal white femininity.</p>
<p>Seen together, the works in these two series offer a unique opportunity to explore the ways in which Thomas interrogates images across subject matter and allows for a complex and nuanced contemplation of the interrelated construction of narratives about race, gender, and class through the vehicle of advertising. By honing in on print advertising, especially drawn from magazines, Thomas also provides an opportunity to reflect on the important role magazines played as a primary form of mass communication during the 20th century.</p>
<p>-----------------</p>
<p><strong>About the Artist:</strong></p>
<p>Hank Willis Thomas earned his BFA in photography and Africana studies from New York University in 1998 and MFA in photography/ MA in visual criticism from California College of Arts, San Francisco in 2004. He is a 2017 recipient of the Open Society Foundations&#8217; Soros Equality Fellowship awarded to practitioners from a variety fields to support work that advances racial justice. He has been a W.E.B. DuBois Institute Resident Fellow at Harvard University, and has received awards and residencies from John Hopkins University, Headlands and Cit&#233; Internationale Universitaire de Paris among others.</p>
<p>----------</p>
<p><em>Hank Willis Thomas: Unbranded</em> is curated by Janet Dees, Steven and Lisa Munster Tananbaum Curator of Modern and Contemporary Art, with assistance from Tamar Kharatishvili, 2017-18 Block Graduate Curatorial Fellow. Funding for this exhibition has been provided by the David C. &amp; Sarajean Ruttenberg Arts Foundation and the Robert Mapplethorpe Foundation.&#160;This program is partially supported by a grant from the Illinois Arts Council Agency.</p>
<p>&#160;</p>			</div>

	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 11th Annual Women's Cardiovascular Health Symposium</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />

	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/520553" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="11th Annual Women's Cardiovascular Health Symposium 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="The aim of this course is to provide up-to-date information about the cardiovascular care of women of all ages for health care providers. On the same day, and often with the same speakers, there is a course with similar topics for the community. The concept is to educate women and their health care providers with much of the same information. The community course is interactive and stresses healthy lifestyle issues. The professional conference is a CME conference assembling experts in the field to discuss the latest advances and research. Both courses have been well received by their respective audiences.
Learn More: https://northwestern.cloud-cme.com/aph.aspx?EID=57029&amp;amp;P=5" />

		<p><span class="label">Group: </span>
		<a href="/calendar/3835" target="_blank">Med - Cardiology</a></p>

		
		<p><span class="label">Category: </span>
		<span class="event_category category_5_txt">Lectures &amp; Meetings</span>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>The aim of this course is to provide up-to-date information about the cardiovascular care of women of all ages for health care providers. On the same day, and often with the same speakers, there is a course with similar topics for the community. The concept is to educate women and their health care providers with much of the same information. The community course is interactive and stresses healthy lifestyle issues. The professional conference is a CME conference assembling experts in the field to discuss the latest advances and research. Both courses have been well received by their respective audiences.</p>
<p>Learn More: https://northwestern.cloud-cme.com/aph.aspx?EID=57029&amp;P=5</p>			</div>

	<meta property="og:description" content="Escaping from the echo chambers of the &quot;Precision Medicine&quot; debate:Can epidemiology and public health be a neutral arbiter? with Richard Cooper
It is widely argued that genomics will continue to revolutionize all of biomedicine by improving diagnosis and treatment &amp;#8211; now designated Precision Medicine (PM). For common disease, where the greatest impact of PM is expected, current experience strongly suggests that prediction of disease risk as well as treatment outcomes for individual patients will not be possible on any large scale. Genome-guided drug development has also been slow and disappointing, limited new insight into pathophysiology has been acquired. Lack of success can be ascribed to several factors. First, the genome is not the domain of interest when the intent is to discover disease causation. Second, the way in which genomic data has been used in clinical research violates several key logical assumptions of epidemiology and clinical trials. Third, the &amp;#8220;reductionist mystique&amp;#8221; has permitted genomics to engage in exaggeration, hype and over-reach, all of which cloud judgment regarding the empirical outcome. A great deal is at stake given the enormous financial and human resources diverted to genomics in recent years and the vast investment in clinical services built around PM. With as many as 50 cohort studies world-wide using whole genome sequence the next 2-3 years will likely be a &amp;#8220;make it or break it&amp;#8221; stage for PM to demonstrate improved clinical outcomes." />

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p><strong>Escaping from the echo chambers of the "Precision Medicine" debate:</strong><br /><strong>Can epidemiology and public health be a neutral arbiter?</strong> with Richard Cooper</p>
<p>It is widely argued that genomics will continue to revolutionize all of biomedicine by improving diagnosis and treatment &#8211; now designated Precision Medicine (PM). For common disease, where the greatest impact of PM is expected, current experience strongly suggests that prediction of disease risk as well as treatment outcomes for individual patients will not be possible on any large scale. Genome-guided drug development has also been slow and disappointing, limited new insight into pathophysiology has been acquired. Lack of success can be ascribed to several factors. First, the genome is not the domain of interest when the intent is to discover disease causation. Second, the way in which genomic data has been used in clinical research violates several key logical assumptions of epidemiology and clinical trials. Third, the &#8220;reductionist mystique&#8221; has permitted genomics to engage in exaggeration, hype and over-reach, all of which cloud judgment regarding the empirical outcome. A great deal is at stake given the enormous financial and human resources diverted to genomics in recent years and the vast investment in clinical services built around PM. With as many as 50 cohort studies world-wide using whole genome sequence the next 2-3 years will likely be a &#8220;make it or break it&#8221; stage for PM to demonstrate improved clinical outcomes.</p>			</div>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Drawing from a decade of fieldwork on Black residents in Hawai&#8217;i,&#160;Nitasha Tamar Sharma (African American Studies)&#160;unsettles the native/settler divide. What emerges from the paradox of ethical Black settlers who draw connections with native Hawaiians while also engaging in the military occupation of Hawai&#8217;i? The overall project animates the Black Pacific and brings Black and Native Studies into conversation through an ethnographic approach.</p>
<p>The Buffett Institute Faculty &amp; Fellows Colloquium brings together an interdisciplinary audience to build awareness of global research on campus. This series promotes dialogue on scholarship and develops a deeper sense of community among Buffett Institute affiliates. Each meeting lasts one hour; lunch is provided. Due to space constraints, we will not admit attendees once the space reaches capacity.</p>			</div>

	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 Technology &amp; Business of Energy (TBOE) Seminar Series</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />
	<link rel="shortcut icon" href="/favicon.ico" type="image/x-icon" />
	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/530053" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="Technology &amp; Business of Energy (TBOE) Seminar Series 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="Are you interested in a career in renewable energy, but unsure where to start? The Technology &amp;amp; Business of Energy (TBOE) Seminars are designed to give you a practical introduction to the basics of the energy industry, as well as serve as a space to network with likeminded students. Northwestern MBA and STEM PhD students will lead broad discussions focusing on the key concepts required to discuss energy intelligently.
Seminars will take place Fridays in Kellogg Global Hub Rm. 2130 from 12:15-1:15pm. Free Krishna lunch (vegetarian Indian boxed lunch) will be provided to registered participants. Participation is open to all NU students (undergraduate, graduate, professional). Please register by February 18th! Register Now here.
*This series is co-sponsored by the Institute for Sustainability and Energy at Northwestern, Kellogg Energy Club, Northwestern Energy and Sustainability Consortium, and SustainNU.
SERIES &amp;#65279;SCHEDULE:
February 23rd:&amp;#160;Energy up to 2010: Fossil Fuels, Hydro and NuclearFossil fuels drove the industrial revolution and rapid economic growth over the last century. The first seminar will introduce why transitioning to cleaner fuel sources is important, and address the difficulties in doing so.
March 2nd: Public Utilities and the Electricity Grid&amp;#160;The public utility market and electricity grid were designed for centralized, reliable production. How will they adapt to address the challenges of renewable energy sources?
March 9th: Energy Efficiency and Government Incentives&amp;#160;Often referred to as the &amp;#8220;first fuel&amp;#8221; in a clean energy economy, energy efficiency lowers energy usage and reduces emissions. Learn about the technology and government programs enabling increased efficiency.
March 16th: Wind and Solar, and Renewable Financing&amp;#160;Wind and Solar are the fastest growing renewable energy sources in the U.S. Learn about what is driving their growth and the economics of clean energy.
April 6th: Startups and Disruption in the Energy Industry&amp;#160;We will hear about the journey and lessons learned from Hazel Technologies LLC, a recent NU sustainability startup. Also, why innovation in the energy industry is largely coming from start-ups rather than utilities.
April 13th: Energy Storage&amp;#160;Intermittency of renewable sources would be far less problematic with scalable energy storage technologies. Learn about what technologies are most promising.
April 20th: Transportation Fuels and Infrastructure&amp;#160;Transportation accounts for about one third of U.S. energy consumption. We will look at the specific economic and technological barriers to transitioning to clean transportation.
April 27th: Energy Policy and the 2017 Tax BillHow will the U.S. tax bill passed at the end of 2017 effect the clean energy economy? Also, an update on how local energy policy has stimulated clean energy growth." />

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Are you interested in a career in renewable energy, but unsure where to start? The Technology &amp; Business of Energy (TBOE) Seminars are designed to give you a practical introduction to the basics of the energy industry, as well as serve as a space to network with likeminded students. Northwestern MBA and STEM PhD students will lead broad discussions focusing on the key concepts required to discuss energy intelligently.</p>
<p>Seminars will take place Fridays in Kellogg Global Hub Rm. 2130 from 12:15-1:15pm. Free Krishna lunch (vegetarian Indian boxed lunch) will be provided to registered participants. Participation is open to all NU students (undergraduate, graduate, professional). <em><span style="text-decoration: underline;">Please register by February 18th</span></em>! Register Now <strong><a href="https://goo.gl/forms/4sOpd5su0LVkbAdm2">here</a></strong>.</p>
<p><em>*This series is co-sponsored by the Institute for Sustainability and Energy at Northwestern, Kellogg Energy Club, Northwestern Energy and Sustainability Consortium, and SustainNU.</em></p>
<p><strong>SERIES &#65279;SCHEDULE:</strong></p>
<p><strong><span style="text-decoration: underline;">Februar</span><span style="text-decoration: underline;">y</span><span style="text-decoration: underline;"> 23rd</span>:</strong>&#160;Energy up to 2010: Fossil Fuels, Hydro and Nuclear<br />Fossil fuels drove the industrial revolution and rapid economic growth over the last century. The first seminar will introduce why transitioning to cleaner fuel sources is important, and address the difficulties in doing so.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong>March 2nd</strong></span>: Public Utilities and the Electricity Grid&#160;<br />The public utility market and electricity grid were designed for centralized, reliable production. How will they adapt to address the challenges of renewable energy sources?</p>
<p><strong><span style="text-decoration: underline;">March 9th</span></strong>: Energy Efficiency and Government Incentives&#160;<br />Often referred to as the &#8220;first fuel&#8221; in a clean energy economy, energy efficiency lowers energy usage and reduces emissions. Learn about the technology and government programs enabling increased efficiency.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong>March 16th</strong></span>: Wind and Solar, and Renewable Financing&#160;<br />Wind and Solar are the fastest growing renewable energy sources in the U.S. Learn about what is driving their growth and the economics of clean energy.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong>April 6th</strong></span>: Startups and Disruption in the Energy Industry&#160;<br />We will hear about the journey and lessons learned from Hazel Technologies LLC, a recent NU sustainability startup. Also, why innovation in the energy industry is largely coming from start-ups rather than utilities.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong>April 13th</strong></span>: Energy Storage&#160;<br />Intermittency of renewable sources would be far less problematic with scalable energy storage technologies. Learn about what technologies are most promising.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong>April 20th</strong></span>: Transportation Fuels and Infrastructure&#160;<br />Transportation accounts for about one third of U.S. energy consumption. We will look at the specific economic and technological barriers to transitioning to clean transportation.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><strong>April 27th</strong></span>: Energy Policy and the 2017 Tax Bill<br />How will the U.S. tax bill passed at the end of 2017 effect the clean energy economy? Also, an update on how local energy policy has stimulated clean energy growth.</p>			</div>

	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 EECS Seminar Speaker: Prof. Jonathan Fan, Assistant Professor in the Dept. of EE at Stanford University, &quot;High Performance Meta-optical Systems for Extreme Waveform Control&quot;</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />
	<link rel="shortcut icon" href="/favicon.ico" type="image/x-icon" />
	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/532964" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="EECS Seminar Speaker: Prof. Jonathan Fan, Assistant Professor in the Dept. of EE at Stanford University, &quot;High Performance Meta-optical Systems for Extreme Waveform Control&quot; 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="The EECS Department welcomes Prof. Jonathan Fan, Assistant Professor in the Dept. of EE at Stanford University.
Fan will present a talk entitled &quot;High Performance Meta-optical Systems for Extreme Waveform Control&quot;&amp;#160;on Friday, April 27 at 2:00 PM in Tech Room L440.
Abstract:&amp;#160;In this talk, I will show that new classes of ultra-high efficiency dielectric metasurfaces, based on topology optimization, can be realized for extreme waveform control. These devices utilize qualitatively new types of light-matter interactions based on strong near-field interactions between dielectric nanostructures, and they enable new diffractive optics phenomena compared to the current state-of-the-art. A theoretical analysis of the supermodes supported by these devices elucidates the underlying physical mechanisms that make such beam deflection possible. To demonstrate the power and versatility of our design approach, I will present devices that can efficiently deflect light to extreme angles, exhibit anomalous refraction, and multiplex different beam steering functions for differing wavelengths. I will also discuss and demonstrate how these concepts can generalize to ultra-high efficiency, large numerical aperture metalenses. We envision that high performance computational design and big data approaches will be essential to defining and designing the next generation of nano-based optical devices.
Bio: Prof. Jonathan Fan is an Assistant Professor in the Department of Electrical Engineering at Stanford University, where he is researching new design methodologies and materials approaches to nanophotonic systems. He received his bachelor&amp;#8217;s degree with highest honors from Princeton University and his doctorate from Harvard University, where he worked with Federico Capasso. He is the recipient of the Air Force Young Investigator Award, Sloan Foundation Fellowship in Physics, Packard Foundation Fellowship, and the Presidential Early Career Award for Scientists and Engineers, which is the highest honor bestowed by the United States government on outstanding scientists and engineers in the early stages of their research careers.
Hosted by EECS Prof. Koray Aydin" />

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>The EECS Department welcomes <strong><a href="https://profiles.stanford.edu/jonathan-fan">Prof. Jonathan Fan</a></strong>, Assistant Professor in the Dept. of EE at Stanford University.</p>
<p>Fan will present a talk entitled "High Performance Meta-optical Systems for Extreme Waveform Control"&#160;on Friday, April 27 at 2:00 PM in Tech Room L440.</p>
<p><strong>Abstract:&#160;</strong>In this talk, I will show that new classes of ultra-high efficiency dielectric metasurfaces, based on topology optimization, can be realized for extreme waveform control. These devices utilize qualitatively new types of light-matter interactions based on strong near-field interactions between dielectric nanostructures, and they enable new diffractive optics phenomena compared to the current state-of-the-art. A theoretical analysis of the supermodes supported by these devices elucidates the underlying physical mechanisms that make such beam deflection possible. To demonstrate the power and versatility of our design approach, I will present devices that can efficiently deflect light to extreme angles, exhibit anomalous refraction, and multiplex different beam steering functions for differing wavelengths. I will also discuss and demonstrate how these concepts can generalize to ultra-high efficiency, large numerical aperture metalenses. We envision that high performance computational design and big data approaches will be essential to defining and designing the next generation of nano-based optical devices.</p>
<p><strong>Bio: Prof. Jonathan Fan </strong><em>is an Assistant Professor in the Department of Electrical Engineering at Stanford University, where he is researching new design methodologies and materials approaches to nanophotonic systems. He received his bachelor&#8217;s degree with highest honors from Princeton University and his doctorate from Harvard University, where he worked with Federico Capasso. He is the recipient of the Air Force Young Investigator Award, Sloan Foundation Fellowship in Physics, Packard Foundation Fellowship, and the Presidential Early Career Award for Scientists and Engineers, which is the highest honor bestowed by the United States government on outstanding scientists and engineers in the early stages of their research careers.</em><strong><br /></strong></p>
<p><strong>Hosted by EECS Prof. Koray Aydin<br /></strong></p>			</div>

<head>
	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 EES Seminar Series: Josiah Hester</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />
	<link rel="shortcut icon" href="/favicon.ico" type="image/x-icon" />
	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/532485" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="EES Seminar Series: Josiah Hester 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="Maintenance free Sensor Networks for Building, Habitat, and Infrastructure Monitoring
Abstract: Wireless sensor networks are groups of small computers dispersed in an area that continuously monitor and record the physical conditions of an environment and wirelessly report that data. These networks, often called &amp;#8220;the Internet-of-Things,&amp;#8221; underly every facet of our lives; ranging from building monitoring systems that sense occupancy and temperature and control HVAC, to habitat and wildlife monitoring systems that observe behaviors of animals and long term changes in the environment. For decades these sensing systems have relied primarily on batteries&amp;#8211;much like the one in our phones&amp;#8211;to power all activities. However, batteries are not a viable energy storage solution for the tiny devices at the edge of a ecologically sustainable Internet of Things. Batteries are expensive, bulky, hazardous, and wear out after a few years (even rechargeables). Replacing and disposing of billions or trillions of dead batteries per year would be expensive and irresponsible. By leaving the batteries behind and surviving off energy harvested from the environment, tiny intermittently powered sensors can monitor objects in hard to reach places maintenance free for decades. Batteryless sensing will revolutionize computing and open up new application domains from infrastructure monitoring and wildlife tracking to wearables, healthcare, and space exploration.  In this talk I will give a brief survey of wireless sensor networks and the Internet-of-Things, highlighting those applications especially relevant to Environmental and Civil Engineering. Then I will discuss my own work moving the sensor networks paradigm towards a sustainable future; including a high level overview of the computational problems that must be overcome, and the platforms I have developed. Finally, I will close with a open-ended discussion of the applications related to health, habitat, and environmental monitoring that this new class of tiny, battery-free computers could enable.
Biography: Josiah Hester is an Assistant Professor in the Department of Electrical Engineering and Computer Science at Northwestern University Josiah joined Northwestern in Fall 2017 after completing his PhD in Computer Science at Clemson University. Josiah&amp;#8217;s research enables sophisticated, sustainable sensing on the tiny devices at the edge of the Internet of Things. These devices enable new application domains from infrastructure monitoring and wildlife tracking to wearables, healthcare, and space exploration. Josiah explores and develops new hardware designs, software techniques, tools, and programming abstractions so that developers can easily design, debug, and deploy intricate batteryless sensor network applications that work in spite of frequent power failures. His work has received a Best Paper Award and Best Paper Nomination from ACM SenSys, and two Best Poster Awards. He was also named the Outstanding PhD Student in Computer Science for 2016 by the School of Computing at Clemson University." />

		<a href="/calendar/897" target="_blank">McCormick - Civil and Environmental Engineering</a></p>

		
		<p><span class="label">Category: </span>
		<span class="event_category category_5_txt">Lectures &amp; Meetings</span>
		</p>

			</div>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p><strong>Maintenance free Sensor Networks for Building, Habitat, and Infrastructure Monitoring</strong></p>
<p><br />Abstract: Wireless sensor networks are groups of small computers dispersed in an area that continuously monitor and record the physical conditions of an environment and wirelessly report that data. These networks, often called &#8220;the Internet-of-Things,&#8221; underly every facet of our lives; ranging from building monitoring systems that sense occupancy and temperature and control HVAC, to habitat and wildlife monitoring systems that observe behaviors of animals and long term changes in the environment. For decades these sensing systems have relied primarily on batteries&#8211;much like the one in our phones&#8211;to power all activities. However, batteries are not a viable energy storage solution for the tiny devices at the edge of a ecologically sustainable Internet of Things. Batteries are expensive, bulky, hazardous, and wear out after a few years (even rechargeables). Replacing and disposing of billions or trillions of dead batteries per year would be expensive and irresponsible. By leaving the batteries behind and surviving off energy harvested from the environment, tiny intermittently powered sensors can monitor objects in hard to reach places maintenance free for decades. Batteryless sensing will revolutionize computing and open up new application domains from infrastructure monitoring and wildlife tracking to wearables, healthcare, and space exploration. <br /> In this talk I will give a brief survey of wireless sensor networks and the Internet-of-Things, highlighting those applications especially relevant to Environmental and Civil Engineering. Then I will discuss my own work moving the sensor networks paradigm towards a sustainable future; including a high level overview of the computational problems that must be overcome, and the platforms I have developed. Finally, I will close with a open-ended discussion of the applications related to health, habitat, and environmental monitoring that this new class of tiny, battery-free computers could enable.</p>
<p><br /><em>Biography: Josiah Hester is an Assistant Professor in the Department of Electrical Engineering and Computer Science at Northwestern University Josiah joined Northwestern in Fall 2017 after completing his PhD in Computer Science at Clemson University. Josiah&#8217;s research enables sophisticated, sustainable sensing on the tiny devices at the edge of the Internet of Things. These devices enable new application domains from infrastructure monitoring and wildlife tracking to wearables, healthcare, and space exploration. Josiah explores and develops new hardware designs, software techniques, tools, and programming abstractions so that developers can easily design, debug, and deploy intricate batteryless sensor network applications that work in spite of frequent power failures. His work has received a Best Paper Award and Best Paper Nomination from ACM SenSys, and two Best Poster Awards. He was also named the Outstanding PhD Student in Computer Science for 2016 by the School of Computing at Clemson University.</em></p>			</div>

<html xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" xml:lang="en" lang="en">
<head>
	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 CHSS Workshop: Lis Clemens, &quot;Public Servants and Citizens, Contractors and Clients: &nbsp;Delegated Governance as a Structure of Exceptions&quot;</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />
	<link rel="shortcut icon" href="/favicon.ico" type="image/x-icon" />
	<meta property="og:url" content="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/533314" />
	<meta property="og:title" content="CHSS Workshop: Lis Clemens, &quot;Public Servants and Citizens, Contractors and Clients: &nbsp;Delegated Governance as a Structure of Exceptions&quot; 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="Elisabeth S. Clemens (A.M. 1985, Ph.D 1990) is a Professor of Sociology at the University of Chicago as well as a former Master of the Social Sciences Collegiate Division. Her research explores the role of social movements and organizational innovation in political change. Clemens' first book, The People's Lobby: Organizational Innovation and the Rise of Interest Group Politics in the United States, 1890-1925 (Chicago, 1997) received best book awards in both organizational sociology (1998) and political sociology (1999). She is also co-editor of Private Action and the Public Good (Yale, 1998), Remaking Modernity: Politics, History and Sociology (Duke, 2005), Politics and Partnerships: Voluntary Associations in America's Past and Present (Chicago, 2010; winner of the 2012 Virginia Hodgkinson Research Prize from ARNOVA), and the journal Studies in American Political Development. She is now completing Civic Nation which traces the tense but powerful entanglements of benevolence and liberalism in the development of the American nation-state.
Professor Clemens will be giving a talk entitled &quot;Public Servants and Citizens, Contractors and Clients: Delegated Governance as a Structure of Exceptions.&quot; An abstract for the project follows:
&quot;The modern state is delineated in many ways:&amp;#160; at the boundaries of formal institutions, through flows of funds and services, or by the distribution of activities over which rights claims are recognized. Publicly-funded and mandated activities may be contracted to private corporations, leaving the employees of those contractors with fewer protections and rights than otherwise equivalent government employees.&amp;#160; Soldiers, sailors and private contractors may fight side-by-side, but their standing in relation to the state differs substantially.&amp;#160; Citizens may be committed to private prisons or care facilities, they may choose to attend publicly-funded but privately-owned and managed schools, or join the millions whose relationship to the Internal Revenue Service is mediated by a privately held corporation providing widely-used tax preparation software. The resulting landscape of exceptions, in which civic rights and political voice are transformed,&amp;#160;obstructed, and constrained by the organization of governance, generates distinctive dynamics of political development.&amp;#160; As political support for explicitly public programs has ebbed and critiques of government have been joined to programs of privatization, opportunities for the exercise of political voice and citizen rights have been refigured in a polity configured of both public and private entities.&quot;" />
	<meta property="og:site_name" content="Northwestern University Events" />
	<meta property="og:image" content="http://www.northwestern.edu/images/northwestern-thumbnail.jpg" />
		<link type="text/css" rel="stylesheet" href="/css/common.css?v=3"/>
	<link type="text/css" rel="stylesheet" href="/css/popup.css?v=3"/>
	<link type="text/css" rel="stylesheet" media="print" href="/css/print.css"/>
	<meta content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0" name="viewport" />
		<link rel="canonical" href="https://planitpurple.northwestern.edu/event/533314" />
</head>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Elisabeth S. Clemens (A.M. 1985, Ph.D 1990) is a Professor of Sociology at the University of Chicago as well as a former Master of the Social Sciences Collegiate Division. Her research explores the role of social movements and organizational innovation in political change. Clemens' first book, The People's Lobby: Organizational Innovation and the Rise of Interest Group Politics in the United States, 1890-1925 (Chicago, 1997) received best book awards in both organizational sociology (1998) and political sociology (1999). She is also co-editor of Private Action and the Public Good (Yale, 1998), Remaking Modernity: Politics, History and Sociology (Duke, 2005), Politics and Partnerships: Voluntary Associations in America's Past and Present (Chicago, 2010; winner of the 2012 Virginia Hodgkinson Research Prize from ARNOVA), and the journal Studies in American Political Development. She is now completing Civic Nation which traces the tense but powerful entanglements of benevolence and liberalism in the development of the American nation-state.</p>
<p>Professor Clemens will be giving a talk entitled "Public Servants and Citizens, Contractors and Clients: Delegated Governance as a Structure of Exceptions." An abstract for the project follows:</p>
<p>"The modern state is delineated in many ways:&#160; at the boundaries of formal institutions, through flows of funds and services, or by the distribution of activities over which rights claims are recognized. Publicly-funded and mandated activities may be contracted to private corporations, leaving the employees of those contractors with fewer protections and rights than otherwise equivalent government employees.&#160; Soldiers, sailors and private contractors may fight side-by-side, but their standing in relation to the state differs substantially.&#160; Citizens may be committed to private prisons or care facilities, they may choose to attend publicly-funded but privately-owned and managed schools, or join the millions whose relationship to the Internal Revenue Service is mediated by a privately held corporation providing widely-used tax preparation software. The resulting landscape of exceptions, in which civic rights and political voice are transformed,&#160;obstructed, and constrained by the organization of governance, generates distinctive dynamics of political development.&#160; As political support for explicitly public programs has ebbed and critiques of government have been joined to programs of privatization, opportunities for the exercise of political voice and citizen rights have been refigured in a polity configured of both public and private entities."</p>			</div>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Title:&#160;Strange metals and black holes</p>
<p>Speaker:Subir Sachdev, Harvard University&#160;</p>
<p>Abstract:&#160;</p>
<p>A `strange metal&#8217; is a new state of matter, formed by electrons in many modern<br />materials. In this state, the electrons quantum entangle with each other across long distances, and conduct electric current collectively (rather than one-by-one, as in an ordinary metal like copper). As they are cooled, most strange metals also become superconductors at relatively high temperatures, in which electric current flows with zero resistance.</p>
<p>Black holes are stars so dense that even light is not able to escape their gravitational attraction. Remarkably, there is a mapping between the quantum theories of these very different physical systems: strange metals and black holes. I will illustrate this equivalence with a very simple model of a strange metal, which can also be viewed as a theory of primitive black hole.</p>
<p>&#160;</p>
<p>Host:</p>
<p>Speaker Schedule</p>
<p>Keywords: Physics, Astronomy, colloquium</p>			</div>

	<title>Northwestern Events Calendar: 04/27/2018 Area X: Environmental Storytelling in the Age of Trump and the Anthropocene (Jeff VanderMeer)</title>
	<meta http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=utf-8" />

	<meta property="og:title" content="Area X: Environmental Storytelling in the Age of Trump and the Anthropocene (Jeff VanderMeer) 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="Tickets for this event are free, but we do ask that you register. Click &quot;Register&quot; box for details.
Jeff VanderMeer, author of Borne and the Southern Reach Trilogy, will discuss the difficulties and the importance of telling stories about the environment during the Anthropocene and in the wake of the 2016 presidential election. In terms ranging from political to personal, VanderMeer will talk about not just the possibility of dystopia but also the need for hope.
Artwork (detail) by Kayla Harrren. Used by permission.
Presented by the Environmental Humanities Research Workshop of the Kaplan Humanities Institute.
The goals of the Environmental Humanities Research Workshop are fairly simple. First and foremost, we hope to create a community of scholars at Northwestern and in the Chicago area who are interested in what we have broadly termed the environmental humanities. We hope that this group will work to define the parameters of the environmental humanities (at least as they exist at Northwestern)&amp;#8212;how and why we as scholars engage with questions of nature, science, ethics, environmental policy and the shifting relationships between the human and the non-human, among many others. To that end, we host informal discussions about provocative pieces of scholarship as well as works-in-progress for interested faculty and graduate students. We also organize public talks by established scholars whose work has helped define and expand humanistic approaches to environmental issues.
To join the Environmental Humanities listserv, please email:Corey Byrnes (corey.byrnes@northwestern.edu) or Keith Woodhouse (keith.woodhouse@northwestern.edu)." />

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>Tickets for this event are free, but we do ask that you register. Click "Register" box for details.</p>
<p>Jeff VanderMeer, author of <em>Borne</em> and the <em>Southern Reach Trilogy</em>, will discuss the difficulties and the importance of telling stories about the environment during the Anthropocene and in the wake of the 2016 presidential election. In terms ranging from political to personal, VanderMeer will talk about not just the possibility of dystopia but also the need for hope.</p>
<p>Artwork (detail) by Kayla Harrren. Used by permission.</p>
<p><em>Presented by the Environmental Humanities Research Workshop of the Kaplan Humanities Institute.</em></p>
<p>The goals of the <strong>Environmental Humanities Research Workshop</strong> are fairly simple. First and foremost, we hope to create a community of scholars at Northwestern and in the Chicago area who are interested in what we have broadly termed the environmental humanities. We hope that this group will work to define the parameters of the environmental humanities (at least as they exist at Northwestern)&#8212;how and why we as scholars engage with questions of nature, science, ethics, environmental policy and the shifting relationships between the human and the non-human, among many others. To that end, we host informal discussions about provocative pieces of scholarship as well as works-in-progress for interested faculty and graduate students. We also organize public talks by established scholars whose work has helped define and expand humanistic approaches to environmental issues.</p>
<p><strong>To join the Environmental Humanities listserv, please email:<br /></strong>Corey Byrnes (<a href="mailto:corey.byrnes@northwestern.edu">corey.byrnes@northwestern.edu</a>) or Keith Woodhouse (<a href="mailto:keith.woodhouse@northwestern.edu">keith.woodhouse@northwestern.edu</a>).</p>			</div>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p><em>Copenhagen</em> is set during World War II, when the friendship of two Nobel Prize-winning physicists is tested by the prospect of providing Nazi Germany with the atomic bomb. A mysterious visit by Werner Heisenberg to Niels Bohr's home in Copenhagen in 1941 brings these renowned physicists and dear friends together under trying circumstances and leads to an argument whose consequences may determine the fate of nations. Uncertainty, the quantum mechanical principle discovered by Heisenberg, lies at every turn as he, Bohr, and Bohr's wife, Margrethe, attempt to reconstruct this fateful conversation and unravel their misunderstanding once and for all. This Tony Award-winning play (2000, Best Play) is based on historical characters and events.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.mccormick.northwestern.edu/etopia/"><strong>Reserve your seat now</strong></a>. Admission is free, but tickets are required to guarantee seating.</p>			</div>

	<meta property="og:title" content="CIERA Astronomer Evenings 04/27/2018" />
	<meta property="og:description" content="CIERA Astronomer Evenings are special programs that take place on the last Friday of every month at Northwestern's Dearborn Observatory. Look through the historic telescope and meet and talk with astronomers from CIERA, Northwestern's Center for Interdisciplinary Exploration and Research in Astrophysics!&amp;#160;The talks are free and open to all.
This month, hear about two space missions in recent news - TESS and Gaia. TESS is a recently launched mission that aims to identify thousands of new exoplanets, with the potential to find hundreds of exoplanets that are (somewhat) similar to Earth. Gaia is an ongoing mission that recently released its first set of results, consisting of accurate three-dimensional positions of over 1 billion(!) stars in our Galaxy, revolutionizing our ability to understand the shape, structure, and history of the Milky Way. We'll discuss why these missions are important, and what we hope to learn from them." />

		<a href="/calendar/3613" target="_blank">CIERA - Center for Interdisciplinary Exploration and Research in Astrophysics</a></p>

		
		<p><span class="label">Category: </span>

					<p><a href="http://ciera.northwestern.edu/AstroEvenings/" target="_blank">More Info</a></p>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>CIERA Astronomer Evenings are special programs that take place on the last Friday of every month at Northwestern's Dearborn Observatory. Look through the historic telescope and meet and talk with astronomers from <a href="http://ciera.northwestern.edu/">CIERA</a>, Northwestern's Center for Interdisciplinary Exploration and Research in Astrophysics!&#160;The talks are free and open to all.</p>
<p>This month, hear about two space missions in recent news - TESS and Gaia. TESS is a recently launched mission that aims to identify thousands of new exoplanets, with the potential to find hundreds of exoplanets that are (somewhat) similar to Earth. Gaia is an ongoing mission that recently released its first set of results, consisting of accurate three-dimensional positions of over 1 billion(!) stars in our Galaxy, revolutionizing our ability to understand the shape, structure, and history of the Milky Way. We'll discuss why these missions are important, and what we hope to learn from them.</p>			</div>

	<a class="detail-button" href="http://www.physics.northwestern.edu/about/dearborn-observatory/" target="_blank">Register</a>

	<div class="event_desc">
		<h3>Description:</h3>
					<p>The Dearborn Observatory is open for public viewing every Friday night from 9 to 11 pm during the spring and summer&#160;months (Apr-Sept). The sessions are free and open to all. Reservations are required for the first hour but walk-ins are welcome in the second hour. All visitors should note that the dome is neither heated nor air-conditioned so please dress appropriately. Friday evening sessions are held "rain or shine." Unfortunately, the Dearborn is NOT ADA-accessible. Several staircases must be climbed in order to reach the telescope. For more information go to http://www.physics.northwestern.edu/about/dearborn-observatory/.</p>
<p>To make a reservation go to http://sites.northwestern.edu/dearborn/.</p>			</div>
